B000JMKTLO|5.0|I absolutely loved Sharp Objects. Murder, suspense, thrillers, and mysteries are typically books I don't read and am very picky about. The authors people rave about are typically my least favorite. I loved Gone Girl and a few people have recommended Gillian Flynn's other books to me, so I grabbed this on a sale table at Barnes and Noble.<br /><br /> Sharp Objects was so twisted. I love the tone and the voice of the narrator, Camille. Gillian Flynn has the ability to make her characters come alive on paper with all of their bitterness, complexity, complexes, issues, and personality. Camille was not just the bitter reporter cookie cutter character, but a real character with problems. When she was advised to head back to her hometown to cover a gruesome story, she reluctantly packed her bags and headed back to place she was happy to be away from.<br /><br /> The story sounds like your average murder thriller movie or book at first glance, but that's what makes Gillian Flynn so talented. She takes an otherwise basic plot and turns it on its head, adding dashes of horror, darkness, insanity, and psychosis. Camille was in town to figure out if the two missing girls were linked, but she also dealt with her own childhood and parental issues while she was in town. The town itself felt suffocating and dull as Camille ran into childhood friends, acquaintances, and their children. Ideas of right and wrong, normal and abnormal, and what sorts of things women should and shouldn't do were tossed around in the novel, which piqued my interest.<br /><br /> The town, the people of the town, the cops, and Camille herself had layers upon layers peeled back in Sharp Objects. For example, at first, the murder looked like two innocent and beautiful girls murdered in cold blood by what the police would assume was a sick man. But as the story progressed, the girls were less and less innocent and sweet, but more troublesome girls who danced to their own beats. And the profile of the killer didn't fit the elegant woman a child last saw one of the girls talking to. Suddenly, a clear cut case with a clear profile of a killer wasn't quite so clear. Nothing really made sense, which brought about questions from Camille about what women might be capable of.<br /><br /> I loved all of the layers and elements of Sharp Objects. Everything was complex. I highly recommend the novel. I thought the overall mystery was fairly easy to unravel later on the in the book, but I think most of that was due to be reading Gone Girl and knowing what sort of twists and directions the author is capable of. Had I not been aware, perhaps the mystery would have been less easy for me to solve. Still, though, it's an awesome and thrilling novel that is truly dark and haunting.

B00BWQW73E|5.0|I have been fascinated by some of the Internet pioneers and in my opinion Jeff Bezos ranks at one of the highest positions. Probably only others who are at this league are Steve Jobs and Bill Gates in terms of the effect on the modern Internet society. Bezos is intriguing as he is not well known to the public and there are no comprehensive biographical sketches. This book has shed immumerable lights on Amazon as a company and on Jeff as a person and his management style. I have been buying from Amazon for past 15 years and is fascinating to see its progress. I commend the author Brad Stone for taking so much time to research and put together a wonderful book.<br /><br />The book does not answer why Jeff Bezos has the most infectious roaring laugh. The roaring laugh one associates with Bezos. Family does not have an answer, but some ex colleagues have. The reasoning is almost counter intuitive, but it sheds into the minds of Bezos. It delves into the interview techniques used at D E Shaw where Bezos worked which Amazon adopted. Some of the ratings are still being practiced at Amazon. Some of the questions asked at Amazon early on e.g., How many fax machines are in the US, gives insight into how candidates think. I am fascinated how Bezos as a geek did not like LaTex, one of the most popular text processing software written by an academic giant Don Knuth of Stanford University. Bezos says, it is not easy to use and brushed aside early on. Well that is true, but it baffles why he said that. Of course Microsoft Word is WYSIWYG. Bezos was hooked to numbers and had awesome insights - a trait for entrepreneur; sometimes he was right, sometimes wrong. He saw a 2300% growth on Internet and hence convinced himself to start Amazon. The book has the one of the first job postings of Amazon in a usenet bulletin board posting in 1994. Seeking C/C++/Unix developer, which is still very much valid 20 years later. Amazon's original names are fascinating. Interesting to note relentless.com still redirects to Amazon. This shows Bezos attachment to his early names!<br /><br />The book goes back and forth between Amazon's early stories, Bezos's personal life and the companies management techniques. Bezos had told investors in early days he projected $74 million in sales in 2000, but actual sales were $1.64 Billion. This and other early day anecdotes show how no one predicted Internet growth. Bezos's childhood days are well captured from the Vanguard program for gifted children at River Oaks Elementary School.<br /><br />One of the most dramatic part of the book is similar to Steve Jobs personal story. 0316204366|2.0|I have to give Mr. Gladwell credit: hes developed a pretty good franchise, and a winning business / publishing formula: (1) develop an interesting sociological thesis that can be distilled into a short/punchy title; (2) identify a number of interesting stories of (mostly unknown) people and situations that support the thesis, and that readers will enjoy sharing at dinner parties; (3) gently weave the stories together in a straightforward and accessible manner, letting the patterns reveal themselves, and then; (4) end it quickly after about 300 pages, but with lots of notes to substantiate it.<br /><br />The only problem with eating at the same franchise too often, is the food starts to lose its appeal. Such is the case with David and Goliath, his fifth restauranter book.  The topic is interesting, but the stories are far too long-winded and at times the connection to the thesis feels tenuous.  This book is not as engaging as his earlier books, which I more wholly enjoyed, and feels like a bit of a contractual obligation.  The sub-title the art of battling giants particularly intrigued me, as I was hopeful that thered be some useful strategies or tactics to be used in battling my own giants. Sadly, thats not the case.  Dyslexia and parental death arent great strategies. Other books, such as Negotiating with Giants, or Judo Strategy, do a much better job of providing specific, practical strategies for getting what you want from much larger, more powerful counterparts. So if youre looking for more science and less art, dont stop at the franchise with the frizzy-haired owner. Try something else.
